EARIN6 - Szymon Gałecki[¶](#EARIN6---Szymon-Ga%C5%82ecki)
---------------------------------------------------------

Program that distinguishes cancer versus normal patterns from mass-spectrometric data based on the dataset:  
[https://archive.ics.uci.edu/ml/datasets/Arcene](https://archive.ics.uci.edu/ml/datasets/Arcene)

In \[ \]:

### 2\. Split data set into training and testing in ratio: 80/20[¶](#2.-Split-data-set-into-training-and-testing-in-ratio:-80/20)

In \[ \]:

from sklearn.model\_selection import train\_test\_split

In \[ \]:

train\_d, test\_d, train\_l, test\_l \= train\_test\_split(td, tl, train\_size\=0.8, random\_state\=0)

In \[ \]:

assert train\_d.shape\[0\] \== train\_l.shape\[0\]
assert test\_d.shape\[0\] \== test\_l.shape\[0\]
assert train\_d.shape\[0\] + test\_d.shape\[0\] \== td.shape\[0\]
assert train\_l.shape\[0\] + test\_l.shape\[0\] \== tl.shape\[0\]

Patterns

valid\_d \= pd.read\_csv('arcene\_valid.data', sep\='\\s+',header\=None)

### 4\. Model: Linear Support Vector Classifier[¶](#4.-Model:-Linear-Support-Vector-Classifier)

Similar to SVC with parameter kernel=’linear’, but implemented in terms of liblinear rather than libsvm, so it has more flexibility in the choice of penalties and loss functions and should scale better to large numbers of samples. This class supports both dense and sparse input and the multiclass support is handled according to a one-vs-the-rest scheme.

In \[ \]:

from sklearn.svm import LinearSVC
from sklearn.metrics import accuracy\_score, confusion\_matrix, ConfusionMatrixDisplay, f1\_score, recall\_score, roc\_auc\_score, plot\_confusion\_matrix
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

In \[ \]:

lsvc \= LinearSVC(random\_state\=0, tol\=1e-5, class\_weight\='balanced')
lsvc.fit(train\_d, train\_l.values.ravel())

Out\[ \]:

LinearSVC(class\_weight='balanced', random\_state=0, tol=1e-05)

Provide metrics for the given model

In \[ \]:

def verification(model, data, labels):
    predicted\_l \= model.predict(data)
    acc \= accuracy\_score(labels, predicted\_l)
    rc \= recall\_score(labels, predicted\_l)
    f1 \= f1\_score(labels, predicted\_l)
    ra \= roc\_auc\_score(labels, predicted\_l)
    
    print(f'Accuracy score - {acc\*100:.2f}%.')
    print(f'Recall score - {rc\*100:.2f}%.')
    print(f'F1 score - {f1\*100:.2f}%.')
    print(f'ROC AUC score - {ra\*100:.2f}%.')

Provide confusion matrix for the given model

In \[ \]:

def confusion\_matrix(model, data, labels):
    disp \= plot\_confusion\_matrix(model, data, labels, normalize\='true', cmap\='Blues')
    disp.ax\_.set\_title('Testing: Confusion matrix - normalized')
    plt.show

verification(lsvc, test\_d, test\_l)

Accuracy score - 80.00%.
Recall score - 62.50%.
F1 score - 71.43%.
ROC AUC score - 77.08%.

In \[ \]:

confusion\_matrix(lsvc, test\_d, test\_l)

#### Remarks:[¶](#Remarks:)

*   Great imbalance between false-positive and false-negative values
*   Significant percent of patients classified as negative were positive, not so good

### 5\. Model: K-Nearest Neighbors[¶](#5.-Model:-K-Nearest-Neighbors)

KNeighborsClassifier implements learning based on the **k** nearest neighbors of each query point, where **k** is an integer value specified by the user.

In \[ \]:

from sklearn.neighbors import KNeighborsClassifier

In \[ \]:

neigh \= KNeighborsClassifier(n\_neighbors\=5)
neigh.fit(train\_d, train\_l.values.ravel())

Out\[ \]:

KNeighborsClassifier()

#### Verification against testing set[¶](#Verification-against-testing-set)

In \[ \]:

verification(neigh, test\_d, test\_l)

Accuracy score - 70.00%.
Recall score - 62.50%.
F1 score - 62.50%.
ROC AUC score - 68.75%.

In \[ \]:

confusion\_matrix(neigh, test\_d, test\_l)

#### Remarks:[¶](#Remarks:)

*   Small percentage of false-negative classifications

### 6\. Model: Random Forest[¶](#6.-Model:-Random-Forest)

A random forest is a meta estimator that fits a number of decision tree classifiers on various sub-samples of the dataset and uses averaging to improve the predictive accuracy and control over-fitting. The sub-sample size is controlled with the `max_samples` parameter if `bootstrap=True` (default), otherwise the whole dataset is used to build each tree.

In \[ \]:

from sklearn.ensemble import RandomForestClassifier

In \[ \]:

rfc \= RandomForestClassifier(random\_state\=0)
rfc.fit(train\_d, train\_l.values.ravel())

Out\[ \]:

RandomForestClassifier(random\_state=0)

#### Verification against testing set[¶](#Verification-against-testing-set)

In \[ \]:

verification(rfc, test\_d, test\_l)

Accuracy score - 75.00%.
Recall score - 62.50%.
F1 score - 66.67%.
ROC AUC score - 72.92%.

In \[ \]:

#### Remarks:[¶](#Remarks:)

*   Very balanced classsification

### 7\. Classifier model comparison[¶](#7.-Classifier-model-comparison)

In \[ \]:

ig, axes \= plt.subplots(nrows\=1, ncols\=3, figsize\=(10,3))
classifiers \= \[lsvc, neigh, rfc\]

for cls, ax in zip(classifiers, axes.flatten()):
    plot\_confusion\_matrix(cls, valid\_d, valid\_l, ax\=ax, cmap\='Blues', normalize\='true')
    ax.title.set\_text(type(cls).\_\_name\_\_)

plt.tight\_layout()  
plt.show()

### Final remarks:[¶](#Final-remarks:)

*   k-nearest neighbors proved to be surprisingly good model for classification despite my assumption that it should be used mainly for data clustering
*   k-nearest neighbors is most prone to overfitting, and was the least consistent model when it comes to accuracy scores
*   random tree-forest was the most consistent model with linear SVC being only slightly worse in that matter
*   while linear SVC was very good overall, the number of false negative values was surprisingly high
*   k-nearest neighbors was the only model independent of its initialisation
